//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b7/Pending-protection-shackle.svg/40px-Pending-protection-shackle.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Immune system</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"><div class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint nomobile" id="mw-fr-revisiontag"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><img alt="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." class="flaggedrevs-icon" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/doc-magnify.png" title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page."/><img alt="show/hide details" class="fr-toggle-arrow" id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/arrow-down.png" style="display:none;"/></div>\n<div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_details" id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" style="display:none">This is the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">latest accepted revision</a>, <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Immune_system">reviewed</a> on <i>18 February 2019</i>.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">A biological system that protects an organism against disease</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neutrophil_with_anthrax_copy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2403" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Neutrophil_with_anthrax_copy.jpg/250px-Neutrophil_with_anthrax_copy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Neutrophil_with_anthrax_copy.jpg/375px-Neutrophil_with_anthrax_copy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Neutrophil_with_anthrax_copy.jpg/500px-Neutrophil_with_anthrax_copy.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neutrophil_with_anthrax_copy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscope" title="Scanning electron microscope">scanning electron microscope</a> image of a single <a href="/wiki/Neutrophil" title="Neutrophil">neutrophil</a> (yellow), engulfing <a href="/wiki/Bacillus_anthracis" title="Bacillus anthracis">anthrax</a> bacteria (orange)</div></div></div>\n<p>The <b>immune system</b> is a host defense system comprising many biological structures and <a href="/wiki/Biological_process" title="Biological process">processes</a> within an <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organism</a> that protects against <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a>. To function properly, an immune system must detect a wide variety of agents, known as <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> to <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_worm" title="Parasitic worm">parasitic worms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Major_histocompatibility_complex" title="Major histocompatibility complex">distinguish</a> them from the organism\'s own healthy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_tissue" title="Biological tissue">tissue</a>. In many species, the immune system can be classified into subsystems, such as the <a href="/wiki/Innate_immune_system" title="Innate immune system">innate immune system</a> versus the <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_immune_system" title="Adaptive immune system">adaptive immune system</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Humoral_immunity" title="Humoral immunity">humoral immunity</a> versus <a href="/wiki/Cell-mediated_immunity" title="Cell-mediated immunity">cell-mediated immunity</a>.  In humans, the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood&#8211;brain barrier">blood&#8211;brain barrier</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93cerebrospinal_fluid_barrier" title="Blood&#8211;cerebrospinal fluid barrier">blood&#8211;cerebrospinal fluid barrier</a>, and similar fluid&#8211;brain barriers separate the peripheral immune system from the <a href="/wiki/Neuroimmune_system" title="Neuroimmune system">neuroimmune system</a>, which protects the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">brain</a>.\n</p><p>Pathogens can rapidly <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolve</a> and adapt, and thereby avoid detection and neutralization by the immune system; however, multiple defense mechanisms have also evolved to recognize and neutralize pathogens. Even simple <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">unicellular</a> organisms such as <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> possess a rudimentary immune system in the form of <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> that protect against <a href="/wiki/Bacteriophage" title="Bacteriophage">bacteriophage</a> infections. Other basic immune mechanisms evolved in ancient <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a> and remain in their modern descendants, such as plants and invertebrates. These mechanisms include <a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis" title="Phagocytosis">phagocytosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_peptides" title="Antimicrobial peptides">antimicrobial peptides</a> called <a href="/wiki/Defensin" title="Defensin">defensins</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Complement_system" title="Complement system">complement system</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jawed_vertebrate" title="Jawed vertebrate">Jawed vertebrates</a>, including humans, have even more sophisticated defense mechanisms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beck_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beck-1">[1]</a></sup> including the ability to adapt over time to recognize specific pathogens more efficiently. Adaptive (or acquired) immunity creates <a href="/wiki/Immunological_memory" title="Immunological memory">immunological memory</a> after an initial response to a specific pathogen, leading to an enhanced response to subsequent encounters with that same pathogen. This process of acquired immunity is the basis of <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a>.\n</p><p>Disorders of the immune system can result in <a href="/wiki/Autoimmune_disease" title="Autoimmune disease">autoimmune diseases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammatory diseases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carcinogenesis" title="Carcinogenesis">cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11506482_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11506482-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Immunodeficiency" title="Immunodeficiency">Immunodeficiency</a> occurs when the immune system is less active than normal, resulting in recurring and life-threatening infections. In humans, immunodeficiency can either be the result of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_disease" title="Genetic disease">genetic disease</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Severe_combined_immunodeficiency" title="Severe combined immunodeficiency">severe combined immunodeficiency</a>, acquired conditions such as <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIDS" title="AIDS">AIDS</a>, or the use of immunosuppressive medication. In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Autoimmunity" title="Autoimmunity">autoimmunity</a> results from a hyperactive immune system attacking normal tissues as if they were foreign organisms. Common autoimmune diseases include <a href="/wiki/Hashimoto%27s_thyroiditis" title="Hashimoto\'s thyroiditis">Hashimoto\'s thyroiditis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rheumatoid_arthritis" title="Rheumatoid arthritis">rheumatoid arthritis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus_type_1" title="Diabetes mellitus type 1">diabetes mellitus type 1</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Systemic_lupus_erythematosus" title="Systemic lupus erythematosus">systemic lupus erythematosus</a>. <a href="/wiki/Immunology" title="Immunology">Immunology</a> covers the study of all aspects of the immune system.\n</p>\n<div tocsection-40"><a href="#Manipulation_by_pathogens"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Manipulation by pathogens</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_immunology">History of immunology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History of immunology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_immunology" title="History of immunology">History of immunology</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Immunology" title="Immunology">Immunology</a> is a science that examines the structure and function of the immune system. It originates from <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a> and early studies on the causes of immunity to disease. The earliest known reference to immunity was during the <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Athens" title="Plague of Athens">plague of Athens</a> in 430 BC. <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a> noted that people who had recovered from a previous bout of the disease could nurse the sick without contracting the illness a second time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> In the 18th century, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Louis_Maupertuis" title="Pierre Louis Maupertuis">Pierre-Louis Moreau de Maupertuis</a> made experiments with scorpion venom and observed that certain <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dogs</a> and mice were immune to this venom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> In the 10th century, Persian physician <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Zakariya_al-Razi" title="Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi">al-Razi</a> (also known as Rhazes) wrote the first recorded theory of acquired immunity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-early_trends_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-early_trends-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-silverstein_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-silverstein-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>6</span></sup> noting that a <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> bout protected its survivors from future infections. Although he explained the immunity in terms of "excess moisture" getting expelled from the blood&#8212;therefore preventing the disease to occur for a second time&#8212;this theory explained many observations about smallpox known during this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-silverstein_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-silverstein-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>7</span></sup>\n</p><p>These and other observations of acquired immunity were later exploited by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> in his development of <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a> and his proposed <a href="/wiki/Germ_theory_of_disease" title="Germ theory of disease">germ theory of disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Pasteur\'s theory was in direct opposition to contemporary theories of disease, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miasma_theory_of_disease" title="Miasma theory of disease">miasma theory</a>. It was not until <a href="/wiki/Robert_Koch" title="Robert Koch">Robert Koch</a>\'s 1891 <a href="/wiki/Koch%27s_postulates" title="Koch\'s postulates">proofs</a>, for which he was awarded a <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize</a> in 1905, that <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> were confirmed as the cause of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">infectious disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Viruses were confirmed as human pathogens in 1901, with the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a> virus by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Reed" title="Walter Reed">Walter Reed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Immunology made a great advance towards the end of the 19th century, through rapid developments, in the study of <a href="/wiki/Humoral_immunity" title="Humoral immunity">humoral immunity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cell-mediated_immunity" title="Cell-mediated immunity">cellular immunity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metch_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metch-10">[10]</a></sup> Particularly important was the work of <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ehrlich" title="Paul Ehrlich">Paul Ehrlich</a>, who proposed the <a href="/wiki/Side-chain_theory" title="Side-chain theory">side-chain theory</a> to explain the specificity of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antigen-antibody_reaction" title="Antigen-antibody reaction">antigen-antibody reaction</a>; his contributions to the understanding of humoral immunity were recognized by the award of a Nobel Prize in 1908, which was jointly awarded to the founder of cellular immunology, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elie_Metchnikoff" title="Elie Metchnikoff">Elie Metchnikoff</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Layered_defense">Layered defense</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Layered defense">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The immune system protects organisms from <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infection</a> with layered defenses of increasing specificity. In simple terms, physical barriers prevent pathogens such as <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> from entering the organism. If a pathogen breaches these barriers, the <a href="/wiki/Innate_immune_system" title="Innate immune system">innate immune system</a> provides an immediate, but non-specific response. Innate immune systems are found in all <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Litman_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Litman-12">[12]</a></sup> If pathogens successfully evade the innate response, vertebrates possess a second layer of protection, the <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_immune_system" title="Adaptive immune system">adaptive immune system</a>, which is activated by the innate response. Here, the immune system adapts its response during an infection to improve its recognition of the pathogen. This improved response is then retained after the pathogen has been eliminated, in the form of an <a href="/wiki/Immunological_memory" title="Immunological memory">immunological memory</a>, and allows the adaptive immune system to mount faster and stronger attacks each time this pathogen is encountered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Restifo_2013_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Restifo_2013-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kurosaki_2015_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kurosaki_2015-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable center" style="margin: 1em auto;">\n<caption><b>Components of the immune system</b>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th style="background:#ccccff;"><a href="#Innate_immune_system">Innate immune system</a></th>\n<th style="background:#ccccff;"><a href="#Adaptive_immune_system">Adaptive immune system</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Response is non-specific</td>\n<td>Pathogen and <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigen</a> specific response\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Exposure leads to immediate maximal response</td>\n<td>Lag time between exposure and maximal response\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Cell-mediated and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humoral_immune_response" title="Humoral immune response">humoral</a> components</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Cell-mediated_immunity" title="Cell-mediated immunity">Cell-mediated</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humoral_immune_response" title="Humoral immune response">humoral</a> components\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>No immunological memory</td>\n<td>Exposure leads to immunological memory\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Found in nearly all forms of life</td>\n<td>Found only in <a href="/wiki/Gnathostomata" title="Gnathostomata">jawed vertebrates</a>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Both innate and adaptive immunity depend on the ability of the immune system to distinguish between self and non-self <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>. In immunology, <i>self</i> molecules are those components of an organism\'s body that can be distinguished from foreign substances by the immune system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Conversely, <i>non-self</i> molecules are those recognized as foreign molecules. One class of non-self molecules are called antigens (short for <i>anti</i>body <i>gen</i>erators) and are defined as substances that bind to specific <a href="/wiki/Immune_receptor" title="Immune receptor">immune receptors</a> and elicit an immune response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberts_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alberts-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Innate_immune_system">Innate immune system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Innate immune system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Innate_immune_system" title="Innate immune system">Innate immune system</a></div>\n<p>Microorganisms or toxins that successfully enter an organism encounter the cells and mechanisms of the innate immune system.  The innate response is usually triggered when microbes are identified by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pattern_recognition_receptors" title="Pattern recognition receptors">pattern recognition receptors</a>, which recognize components that are conserved among broad groups of microorganisms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17943118_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17943118-17">[17]</a></sup> or when damaged, injured or stressed cells send out alarm signals, many of which (but not all) are recognized by the same receptors as those that recognize pathogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11951032_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11951032-18">[18]</a></sup> Innate immune defenses are non-specific, meaning these systems respond to pathogens in a generic way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberts_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alberts-16">[16]</a></sup> This system does not confer long-lasting <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a> against a pathogen. The innate immune system is the dominant system of host defense in most organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Litman_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Litman-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_barriers">Surface barriers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Surface barriers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Several barriers protect organisms from infection, including mechanical, chemical, and biological barriers. The waxy <a href="/wiki/Plant_cuticle" title="Plant cuticle">cuticle</a> of most <a href="/wiki/Leaf" title="Leaf">leaves</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">exoskeleton</a> of <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eggshell" title="Eggshell">shells</a> and membranes of externally deposited <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" title="Egg (biology)">eggs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Skin" title="Skin">skin</a> are examples of mechanical barriers that are the first line of defense against infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberts_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Alberts-16">[16]</a></sup> However, as organisms cannot be completely sealed from their environments, other systems act to protect body openings such as the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestine" title="Intestine">intestines</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Genitourinary_system" title="Genitourinary system">genitourinary tract</a>. In the lungs, <a href="/wiki/Cough" title="Cough">coughing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sneeze" title="Sneeze">sneezing</a> mechanically eject pathogens and other <a href="/wiki/Irritation" title="Irritation">irritants</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">respiratory tract</a>. The flushing action of <a href="/wiki/Tears" title="Tears">tears</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a> also mechanically expels pathogens, while <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a> secreted by the respiratory and <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">gastrointestinal tract</a> serves to trap and entangle microorganisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Chemical barriers also protect against infection. The skin and respiratory tract secrete <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_peptides" title="Antimicrobial peptides">antimicrobial peptides</a> such as the &#946;-<a href="/wiki/Defensin" title="Defensin">defensins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">Enzymes</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Lysozyme" title="Lysozyme">lysozyme</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phospholipase_A2" title="Phospholipase A2">phospholipase A2</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saliva" title="Saliva">saliva</a>, tears, and <a href="/wiki/Breast_milk" title="Breast milk">breast milk</a> are also <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antibacterials</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vagina" title="Vagina">Vaginal</a> secretions serve as a chemical barrier following <a href="/wiki/Menarche" title="Menarche">menarche</a>, when they become slightly <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acidic</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Semen" title="Semen">semen</a> contains defensins and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> to kill pathogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">stomach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gastric_acid" title="Gastric acid">gastric acid</a> serves as a powerful chemical defense against ingested pathogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Within the genitourinary and gastrointestinal tracts, <a href="/wiki/Commensalism" title="Commensalism">commensal</a> <a href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">flora</a> serve as biological barriers by competing with pathogenic bacteria for food and space and, in some cases, by changing the conditions in their environment, such as <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> or available iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> As a result of the <a href="/wiki/Microbial_symbiosis_and_immunity" title="Microbial symbiosis and immunity">symbiotic relationship between commensals and the immune system</a>, the probability that pathogens will reach sufficient numbers to cause illness is reduced. However, since most <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> non-specifically target bacteria and do not affect fungi, oral antibiotics can lead to an "overgrowth" of <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a> and cause conditions such as a vaginal <a href="/wiki/Candidiasis" title="Candidiasis">candidiasis</a> (a yeast infection).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> There is good evidence that re-introduction of <a href="/wiki/Probiotic" title="Probiotic">probiotic</a> flora, such as pure cultures of the <a href="/wiki/Lactobacillus" title="Lactobacillus">lactobacilli</a> normally found in unpasteurized <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>, helps restore a healthy balance of microbial populations in intestinal infections in children and encouraging preliminary data in studies on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacterial_gastroenteritis" title="Bacterial gastroenteritis">bacterial gastroenteritis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inflammatory_bowel_disease" title="Inflammatory bowel disease">inflammatory bowel diseases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urinary_tract_infection" title="Urinary tract infection">urinary tract infection</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-surgical_infections" title="Post-surgical infections">post-surgical infections</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inflammation">Inflammation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Inflammation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">Inflammation</a></div>\n<p>Inflammation is one of the first responses of the immune system to infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-31">[31]</a></sup> The symptoms of inflammation are redness, swelling, heat, and pain, which are caused by increased <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> flow into tissue. Inflammation is produced by <a href="/wiki/Eicosanoid" title="Eicosanoid">eicosanoids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a>, which are released by injured or infected cells. Eicosanoids include <a href="/wiki/Prostaglandin" title="Prostaglandin">prostaglandins</a> that produce <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vasodilator" title="Vasodilator">dilation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a> associated with inflammation, and <a href="/wiki/Leukotriene" title="Leukotriene">leukotrienes</a> that attract certain <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cells</a> (leukocytes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-33">[33]</a></sup> Common cytokines include <a href="/wiki/Interleukin" title="Interleukin">interleukins</a> that are responsible for communication between white blood cells; <a href="/wiki/Chemokine" title="Chemokine">chemokines</a> that promote <a href="/wiki/Chemotaxis" title="Chemotaxis">chemotaxis</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Interferon" title="Interferon">interferons</a> that have <a href="/wiki/Antiviral_drug" title="Antiviral drug">anti-viral</a> effects, such as shutting down <a href="/wiki/Protein_biosynthesis" title="Protein biosynthesis">protein synthesis</a> in the host cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Growth_factor" title="Growth factor">Growth factors</a> and cytotoxic factors may also be released. These cytokines and other chemicals recruit immune cells to the site of infection and promote healing of any damaged tissue following the removal of pathogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complement_system">Complement system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Complement system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Complement_system" title="Complement system">Complement system</a></div>\n<p>The complement system is a <a href="/wiki/Biochemical_cascade" title="Biochemical cascade">biochemical cascade</a> that attacks the surfaces of foreign cells. It contains over 20 different proteins and is named for its ability to "complement" the killing of pathogens by <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a>. Complement is the major humoral component of the innate immune response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rus_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rus-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Degn_2013_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Degn_2013-37">[37]</a></sup> Many species have complement systems, including non-<a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> like plants, fish, and some <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janeway6_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway6-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In humans, this response is activated by complement binding to antibodies that have attached to these microbes or the binding of complement proteins to <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> on the surfaces of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbe" title="Microbe">microbes</a>. This recognition <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">signal</a> triggers a rapid killing response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The speed of the response is a result of signal amplification that occurs after sequential <a href="/wiki/Proteolysis" title="Proteolysis">proteolytic</a> activation of complement molecules, which are also proteases. After complement proteins initially bind to the microbe, they activate their protease activity, which in turn activates other complement proteases, and so on. This produces a <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalytic</a> cascade that amplifies the initial signal by controlled <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The cascade results in the production of peptides that attract immune cells, increase <a href="/wiki/Vascular_permeability" title="Vascular permeability">vascular permeability</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Opsonin" title="Opsonin">opsonize</a> (coat) the surface of a pathogen, marking it for destruction. This deposition of complement can also kill cells directly by disrupting their <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">plasma membrane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rus_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rus-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cellular_barriers">Cellular barriers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cellular barriers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SEM_blood_cells.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2239" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/SEM_blood_cells.jpg/220px-SEM_blood_cells.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/SEM_blood_cells.jpg/330px-SEM_blood_cells.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/SEM_blood_cells.jpg/440px-SEM_blood_cells.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SEM_blood_cells.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscope" title="Scanning electron microscope">scanning electron microscope</a> image of normal circulating human <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a>. One can see <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>, several knobby white blood cells including <a href="/wiki/Lymphocyte" title="Lymphocyte">lymphocytes</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Monocyte" title="Monocyte">monocyte</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Neutrophil" title="Neutrophil">neutrophil</a>, and many small disc-shaped <a href="/wiki/Platelet" title="Platelet">platelets</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Leukocytes (white blood cells) act like independent, single-celled organisms and are the second arm of the innate immune system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberts_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Alberts-16">[16]</a></sup> The innate leukocytes include the <a href="/wiki/Phagocyte" title="Phagocyte">phagocytes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrophil_granulocyte" title="Neutrophil granulocyte">neutrophils</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dendritic_cell" title="Dendritic cell">dendritic cells</a>), <a href="/wiki/Innate_lymphoid_cell" title="Innate lymphoid cell">innate lymphoid cells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mast_cell" title="Mast cell">mast cells</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eosinophil_granulocyte" title="Eosinophil granulocyte">eosinophils</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basophil_granulocyte" title="Basophil granulocyte">basophils</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Natural_killer_cell" title="Natural killer cell">natural killer cells</a>. These cells identify and eliminate pathogens, either by attacking larger pathogens through contact or by engulfing and then killing microorganisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janeway6_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway6-38">[38]</a></sup> Innate cells are also important mediators in lymphoid organ development and the activation of the adaptive immune system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Withers_2016_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Withers_2016-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis" title="Phagocytosis">Phagocytosis</a> is an important feature of cellular innate immunity performed by cells called phagocytes that engulf, or eat, pathogens or particles. Phagocytes generally patrol the body searching for pathogens, but can be called to specific locations by cytokines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberts_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Alberts-16">[16]</a></sup> Once a pathogen has been engulfed by a phagocyte, it becomes trapped in an intracellular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesicle_(biology)" title="Vesicle (biology)">vesicle</a> called a <a href="/wiki/Phagosome" title="Phagosome">phagosome</a>, which subsequently fuses with another vesicle called a <a href="/wiki/Lysosome" title="Lysosome">lysosome</a> to form a <a href="/wiki/Phagolysosome" title="Phagolysosome">phagolysosome</a>. The pathogen is killed by the activity of digestive enzymes or following a <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_burst" title="Respiratory burst">respiratory burst</a> that releases <a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)" title="Radical (chemistry)">free radicals</a> into the phagolysosome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Phagocytosis evolved as a means of acquiring <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a>, but this role was extended in phagocytes to include engulfment of pathogens as a defense mechanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Phagocytosis probably represents the oldest form of host defense, as phagocytes have been identified in both vertebrate and invertebrate animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Phagocytes">Phagocytes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Phagocytes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Phagocyte" title="Phagocyte">Phagocyte</a></div>\n<p>Neutrophils and macrophages are phagocytes that travel throughout the body in pursuit of invading pathogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Neutrophils are normally found in the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">bloodstream</a> and are the most abundant type of phagocyte, normally representing 50% to 60% of the total circulating leukocytes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IandF_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-IandF-47">[47]</a></sup> and consisting of neutrophil-killer and neutrophil-cager <a href="/wiki/Neutrophil#Subpopulations" title="Neutrophil">subpopulations</a>. During the acute phase of inflammation, particularly as a result of bacterial infection, neutrophils migrate toward the site of inflammation in a process called chemotaxis, and are usually the first cells to arrive at the scene of infection. Macrophages are versatile cells that reside within tissues and produce a wide array of chemicals including enzymes, <a href="/wiki/Complement_system" title="Complement system">complement proteins</a>, and cytokines, while they can also act as scavengers that rid the body of worn-out cells and other debris, and as <a href="/wiki/Antigen-presenting_cell" title="Antigen-presenting cell">antigen-presenting cells</a> that activate the adaptive immune system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rua_2015_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rua_2015-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dendritic_cells">Dendritic cells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Dendritic cells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dendritic_cell" title="Dendritic cell">Dendritic cell</a></div>\n<p>Dendritic cells (DC) are phagocytes in tissues that are in contact with the external environment; therefore, they are located mainly in the <a href="/wiki/Human_skin" title="Human skin">skin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_nose" title="Human nose">nose</a>, lungs, stomach, and intestines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guermonprez_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guermonprez-49">[49]</a></sup> They are named for their resemblance to <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neuronal</a> <a href="/wiki/Dendrite" title="Dendrite">dendrites</a>, as both have many spine-like projections, but dendritic cells are in no way connected to the <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a>. Dendritic cells serve as a link between the bodily tissues and the innate and adaptive immune systems, as they <a href="/wiki/Antigen_presentation" title="Antigen presentation">present antigens</a> to <a href="/wiki/T_cell" title="T cell">T cells</a>, one of the key cell types of the adaptive immune system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guermonprez_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guermonprez-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Granulocytes">Granulocytes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Granulocytes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Granulocyte" title="Granulocyte">Granulocyte</a></div>\n<p>Mast cells reside in <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissues</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucous membranes</a>, and regulate the inflammatory response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> They are most often associated with <a href="/wiki/Allergy" title="Allergy">allergy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylaxis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IandF_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-IandF-47">[47]</a></sup> Basophils and eosinophils are related to neutrophils. They secrete chemical mediators that are involved in defending against <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasites</a> and play a role in allergic reactions, such as <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_killer_cells">Natural killer cells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Natural killer cells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Natural_killer_cell" title="Natural killer cell">Natural killer cell</a></div>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_killer_cells" title="Natural killer cells">Natural killer cells</a>, or NK cells, are lymphocytes and a component of the innate immune system which does not directly attack invading microbes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid28078307_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid28078307-52">[52]</a></sup>  Rather, NK cells destroy compromised host cells, such as tumor cells or virus-infected cells, recognizing such cells by a condition known as "missing self." This term describes cells with low levels of a cell-surface marker called MHC I (<a href="/wiki/Major_histocompatibility_complex" title="Major histocompatibility complex">major histocompatibility complex</a>)&#8212;a situation that can arise in viral infections of host cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janeway6_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway6-38">[38]</a></sup> They were named "natural killer" because of the initial notion that they do not require activation in order to kill cells that are "missing self." For many years it was unclear how NK cells recognize tumor cells and infected cells. It is now known that the MHC makeup on the surface of those cells is altered and the NK cells become activated through recognition of "missing self".  Normal body cells are not recognized and attacked by NK cells because they express intact self MHC antigens. Those MHC antigens are recognized by killer cell immunoglobulin receptors (KIR) which essentially put the brakes on NK cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22665247_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22665247-53">[53]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adaptive_immune_system">Adaptive immune system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Adaptive immune system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_immune_system" title="Adaptive immune system">Adaptive immune system</a></div>\n<p>The adaptive immune system evolved in early vertebrates and allows for a stronger immune response as well as immunological memory, where each pathogen is "remembered" by a signature antigen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The adaptive immune response is antigen-specific and requires the recognition of specific "non-self" antigens during a process called antigen presentation. Antigen specificity allows for the generation of responses that are tailored to specific pathogens or pathogen-infected cells. The ability to mount these tailored responses is maintained in the body by "memory cells". Should a pathogen infect the body more than once, these specific memory cells are used to quickly eliminate it.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lymphocytes">Lymphocytes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Lymphocytes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The cells of the adaptive immune system are special types of leukocytes, called lymphocytes. <a href="/wiki/B_cell" title="B cell">B cells</a> and T cells are the major types of lymphocytes and are derived from <a href="/wiki/Hematopoietic_stem_cell" title="Hematopoietic stem cell">hematopoietic stem cells</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janeway6_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway6-38">[38]</a></sup> B cells are involved in the <a href="/wiki/Humoral_immunity" title="Humoral immunity">humoral immune response</a>, whereas T cells are involved in <a href="/wiki/Cell-mediated_immunity" title="Cell-mediated immunity">cell-mediated immune response</a>.\n</p><p>Both B cells and T cells carry receptor molecules that recognize specific targets. T cells recognize a "non-self" target, such as a pathogen, only after antigens (small fragments of the pathogen) have been processed and presented in combination with a "self" receptor called a major histocompatibility complex (MHC) molecule. There are two major subtypes of T cells: the <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxic_T_cell" title="Cytotoxic T cell">killer T cell</a> and the <a href="/wiki/T_helper_cell" title="T helper cell">helper T cell</a>. In addition there are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regulatory_T_cells" title="Regulatory T cells">regulatory T cells</a> which  have a role in modulating immune response. Killer T cells only recognize antigens coupled to <a href="/wiki/Major_histocompatibility_complex#MHC_class_I" title="Major histocompatibility complex">Class I MHC</a> molecules, while helper T cells and regulatory T cells only recognize antigens coupled to <a href="/wiki/Major_histocompatibility_complex#MHC_class_II" title="Major histocompatibility complex">Class II MHC</a> molecules. These two mechanisms of antigen presentation reflect the different roles of the two types of T cell. A third, minor subtype are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma/delta_T_cells" title="Gamma/delta T cells">&#947;&#948; T cells</a> that recognize intact antigens that are not bound to MHC receptors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holtmeier_W,_Kabelitz_D_2005_151&#8211;83_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holtmeier_W,_Kabelitz_D_2005_151&#8211;83-55">[55]</a></sup> The double-positive T cells are exposed to a wide variety of self-antigens in the <a href="/wiki/Thymus" title="Thymus">thymus</a>,  in which <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a> is necessary for its thymus development and activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19647627_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19647627-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In contrast, the B cell antigen-specific receptor is an <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibody</a> molecule on the B cell surface, and recognizes whole pathogens without any need for <a href="/wiki/Antigen_processing" title="Antigen processing">antigen processing</a>. Each lineage of B cell expresses a different antibody, so the complete set of B cell antigen receptors represent all the antibodies that the body can manufacture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janeway6_38-4"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway6-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Killer_T_cells">Killer T cells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Killer T cells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Cytotoxic_T_cell" title="Cytotoxic T cell">Killer T cells</a> are a sub-group of T cells that kill cells that are infected with viruses (and other pathogens), or are otherwise damaged or dysfunctional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> As with B cells, each type of T cell recognizes a different antigen. Killer T cells are activated when their <a href="/wiki/T-cell_receptor" title="T-cell receptor">T-cell receptor</a> (TCR) binds to this specific antigen in a complex with the MHC Class I receptor of another cell. Recognition of this MHC:antigen complex is aided by a <a href="/wiki/Co-receptor" title="Co-receptor">co-receptor</a> on the T cell, called <a href="/wiki/CD8" title="CD8">CD8</a>. The T cell then travels throughout the body in search of cells where the MHC I receptors bear this antigen. When an activated T cell contacts such cells, it releases <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxicity" title="Cytotoxicity">cytotoxins</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Perforin" title="Perforin">perforin</a>, which form pores in the target cell\'s <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">plasma membrane</a>, allowing <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a>, water and toxins to enter. The entry of another toxin called <a href="/wiki/Granulysin" title="Granulysin">granulysin</a> (a protease) induces the target cell to undergo <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radoja_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Radoja-58">[58]</a></sup> T cell killing of host cells is particularly important in preventing the replication of viruses. T cell activation is tightly controlled and generally requires a very strong MHC/antigen activation signal, or additional activation signals provided by "helper" T cells (see below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radoja_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Radoja-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Helper_T_cells">Helper T cells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Helper T cells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lymphocyte_activation_simple.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="358" data-file-width="612" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Lymphocyte_activation_simple.png/300px-Lymphocyte_activation_simple.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Lymphocyte_activation_simple.png/450px-Lymphocyte_activation_simple.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Lymphocyte_activation_simple.png/600px-Lymphocyte_activation_simple.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lymphocyte_activation_simple.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Function of T helper cells: Antigen-presenting cells (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antigen_presenting_cell" title="Antigen presenting cell">APCs</a>) present antigen on their Class II MHC molecules (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Class_II_MHC_molecule" title="Class II MHC molecule">MHC2</a>). Helper T cells recognize these, with the help of their expression of CD4 co-receptor (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CD4%2B" title="CD4+">CD4+</a>). The activation of a resting helper T cell causes it to release cytokines and other stimulatory signals (green arrows) that stimulate the activity of <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxic_T_cell" title="Cytotoxic T cell">killer T cells</a> and <a href="/wiki/B_cell" title="B cell">B cells</a>, the latter producing <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a>. The stimulation of B cells and macrophages succeeds a proliferation of T helper cells.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/T_helper_cell" title="T helper cell">Helper T cells</a> regulate both the innate and adaptive immune responses and help determine which immune responses the body makes to a particular pathogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> These cells have no cytotoxic activity and do not kill infected cells or clear pathogens directly. They instead control the immune response by directing other cells to perform these tasks.\n</p><p>Helper T cells express T cell receptors (TCR) that recognize antigen bound to Class II MHC molecules. The MHC:antigen complex is also recognized by the helper cell\'s <a href="/wiki/CD4" title="CD4">CD4</a> co-receptor, which recruits molecules inside the T cell (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Lck" title="Lck">Lck</a>) that are responsible for the T cell\'s activation. Helper T cells have a weaker association with the MHC:antigen complex than observed for killer T cells, meaning many receptors (around 200&#8211;300) on the helper T cell must be bound by an MHC:antigen in order to activate the helper cell, while killer T cells can be activated by engagement of a single MHC:antigen molecule. Helper T cell activation also requires longer duration of engagement with an antigen-presenting cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The activation of a resting helper T cell causes it to release cytokines that influence the activity of many cell types. Cytokine signals produced by helper T cells enhance the microbicidal function of macrophages and the activity of killer T cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberts_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-Alberts-16">[16]</a></sup> In addition, helper T cell activation causes an upregulation of molecules expressed on the T cell\'s surface, such as CD40 ligand (also called <a href="/wiki/CD154" title="CD154">CD154</a>), which provide extra stimulatory signals typically required to activate antibody-producing B cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gamma_delta_T_cells">Gamma delta T cells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Gamma delta T cells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Gamma_delta_T_cell" title="Gamma delta T cell">Gamma delta T cells</a> <b>(&#947;&#948; T cells)</b> possess an alternative <a href="/wiki/T-cell_receptor" title="T-cell receptor">T-cell receptor</a> (TCR) as opposed to CD4+ and CD8+ (&#945;&#946;) T cells and share the characteristics of helper T cells, cytotoxic T cells and NK cells. The conditions that produce responses from &#947;&#948; T cells are not fully understood. Like other \'unconventional\' T cell subsets bearing invariant TCRs, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CD1d_receptor" title="CD1d receptor">CD1d</a>-restricted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_Killer_T_cell" title="Natural Killer T cell">Natural Killer T cells</a>, &#947;&#948; T cells straddle the border between innate and adaptive immunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> On one hand, &#947;&#948; T cells are a component of <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_immune_system" title="Adaptive immune system">adaptive immunity</a> as they <a href="/wiki/V(D)J_recombination" title="V(D)J recombination">rearrange TCR genes</a> to produce receptor diversity and can also develop a memory phenotype. On the other hand, the various subsets are also part of the innate immune system, as restricted TCR or NK receptors may be used as <a href="/wiki/Pattern_recognition_receptor" title="Pattern recognition receptor">pattern recognition receptors</a>. For example, large numbers of human V&#947;9/V&#948;2 T cells respond within hours to <a href="/wiki/Non-peptidic_antigen" title="Non-peptidic antigen">common molecules</a> produced by microbes, and highly restricted V&#948;1+ T cells in <a href="/wiki/Epithelium" title="Epithelium">epithelia</a> respond to stressed epithelial cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holtmeier_W,_Kabelitz_D_2005_151&#8211;83_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holtmeier_W,_Kabelitz_D_2005_151&#8211;83-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antibody_svg.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="313" data-file-width="339" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Antibody_svg.svg/220px-Antibody_svg.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Antibody_svg.svg/330px-Antibody_svg.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Antibody_svg.svg/440px-Antibody_svg.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antibody_svg.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An antibody is made up of two heavy chains and two light chains. The unique variable region allows an antibody to recognize its matching antigen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIAID_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIAID-64">[64]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="B_lymphocytes_and_antibodies">B lymphocytes and antibodies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: B lymphocytes and antibodies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/B_cell" title="B cell">B cell</a> identifies pathogens when antibodies on its surface bind to a specific foreign antigen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sproul_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sproul-65">[65]</a></sup> This antigen/antibody complex is taken up by the B cell and processed by <a href="/wiki/Proteolysis" title="Proteolysis">proteolysis</a> into peptides. The B cell then displays these antigenic peptides on its surface MHC class II molecules. This combination of MHC and antigen attracts a matching helper T cell, which releases <a href="/wiki/Lymphokine" title="Lymphokine">lymphokines</a> and activates the B cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> As the activated B cell then begins to <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">divide</a>, its offspring (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_cells" title="Plasma cells">plasma cells</a>) <a href="/wiki/Secretion" title="Secretion">secrete</a> millions of copies of the antibody that recognizes this antigen. These antibodies circulate in blood plasma and <a href="/wiki/Lymphatic_system" title="Lymphatic system">lymph</a>, bind to pathogens expressing the antigen and mark them for destruction by <a href="#Complement_system">complement activation</a> or for uptake and destruction by phagocytes. Antibodies can also neutralize challenges directly, by binding to bacterial toxins or by interfering with the receptors that viruses and bacteria use to infect cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_adaptive_immune_system">Alternative adaptive immune system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Alternative adaptive immune system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Adaptive_immune_system#evolution" title="Adaptive immune system">Evolution of the adaptive immune system</a> occurred in an ancestor of the jawed vertebrates. Many of the classical molecules of the adaptive immune system (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin" title="Immunoglobulin">immunoglobulins</a> and <a href="/wiki/T-cell_receptor" title="T-cell receptor">T-cell receptors</a>) exist only in jawed vertebrates. However, a distinct <a href="/wiki/Lymphocyte" title="Lymphocyte">lymphocyte</a>-derived molecule has been discovered in primitive <a href="/wiki/Agnatha" title="Agnatha">jawless vertebrates</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lamprey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hagfish" title="Hagfish">hagfish</a>. These animals possess a large array of molecules called <a href="/wiki/Variable_lymphocyte_receptor" title="Variable lymphocyte receptor">Variable lymphocyte receptors</a> (VLRs) that, like the antigen receptors of jawed vertebrates, are produced from only a small number (one or two) of <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>. These molecules are believed to bind pathogenic <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigens</a> in a similar way to antibodies, and with the same degree of specificity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immunological_memory">Immunological memory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Immunological memory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">Immunity (medical)</a></div>\n<p>When B cells and T cells are activated and begin to replicate, some of their offspring become long-lived memory cells. Throughout the lifetime of an animal, these memory cells remember each specific pathogen encountered and can mount a strong response if the pathogen is detected again. This is "adaptive" because it occurs during the lifetime of an individual as an adaptation to infection with that pathogen and prepares the immune system for future challenges. Immunological memory can be in the form of either passive short-term memory or active long-term memory.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Passive_memory">Passive memory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Passive memory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Newborn <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">infants</a> have no prior exposure to microbes and are particularly vulnerable to infection. Several layers of passive protection are provided by the mother.   During <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a>, a particular type of antibody, called <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_G" title="Immunoglobulin G">IgG</a>, is transported from mother to baby directly through the <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a>, so human babies have high levels of antibodies even at birth, with the same range of antigen specificities as their mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Breast_milk" title="Breast milk">Breast milk</a> or  <a href="/wiki/Colostrum" title="Colostrum">colostrum</a> also contains antibodies that are transferred to the gut of the infant and protect against bacterial infections until the newborn can synthesize its own antibodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> This is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passive_immunization" title="Passive immunization">passive immunity</a> because the <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetus</a> does not actually make any memory cells or antibodies&#8212;it only borrows them. This passive immunity is usually short-term, lasting from a few days up to several months. In medicine, protective passive immunity can also be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intravenous_immunoglobulin" title="Intravenous immunoglobulin">transferred artificially</a> from one individual to another via antibody-rich <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">serum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keller_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keller-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Immune_response2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="273" data-file-width="395" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Immune_response2.svg/360px-Immune_response2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Immune_response2.svg/540px-Immune_response2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Immune_response2.svg/720px-Immune_response2.svg.png 2x" width="360"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Immune_response2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The time-course of an immune response begins with the initial pathogen encounter, (or initial vaccination) and leads to the formation and maintenance of active immunological memory.</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Active_memory_and_immunization">Active memory and immunization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Active memory and immunization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Long-term <i>active</i> memory is acquired following infection by activation of B and T cells. Active immunity can also be generated artificially, through <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a>. The principle behind vaccination (also called <a href="/wiki/Immunization" title="Immunization">immunization</a>) is to introduce an <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigen</a> from a pathogen in order to stimulate the immune system and develop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_immunity" title="Specific immunity">specific immunity</a> against that particular pathogen without causing disease associated with that organism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberts_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-Alberts-16">[16]</a></sup> This deliberate induction of an immune response is successful because it exploits the natural specificity of the immune system, as well as its inducibility. With infectious disease remaining one of the leading causes of death in the human population, vaccination represents the most effective manipulation of the immune system mankind has developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janeway6_38-5"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway6-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most viral <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a> are based on live <a href="/wiki/Attenuator_(genetics)" title="Attenuator (genetics)">attenuated</a> viruses, while many bacterial vaccines are based on <a href="/wiki/Non-cellular_life" title="Non-cellular life">acellular</a> components of micro-organisms, including harmless <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxin</a> components.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberts_16-7"><a href="#cite_note-Alberts-16">[16]</a></sup> Since many antigens derived from acellular vaccines do not strongly induce the adaptive response, most bacterial vaccines are provided with additional <a href="/wiki/Immunologic_adjuvant" title="Immunologic adjuvant">adjuvants</a> that activate the <a href="/wiki/Antigen-presenting_cell" title="Antigen-presenting cell">antigen-presenting cells</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system#Innate" title="Immune system">innate immune system</a> and maximize <a href="/wiki/Immunogenicity" title="Immunogenicity">immunogenicity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Disorders_of_human_immunity">Disorders of human immunity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Disorders of human immunity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The immune system is a remarkably effective structure that incorporates specificity, inducibility and adaptation. Failures of host defense do occur, however, and fall into three broad categories: immunodeficiencies, autoimmunity, and hypersensitivities.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immunodeficiencies">Immunodeficiencies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Immunodeficiencies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Immunodeficiency" title="Immunodeficiency">Immunodeficiency</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Immunodeficiency" title="Immunodeficiency">Immunodeficiencies</a> occur when one or more of the components of the immune system are inactive. The ability of the immune system to respond to pathogens is diminished in both the young and the <a href="/wiki/Old_age" title="Old age">elderly</a>, with immune responses beginning to decline at around 50 years of age due to <a href="/wiki/Immunosenescence" title="Immunosenescence">immunosenescence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nutrition_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-nutrition-75">[75]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed countries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_abuse" title="Alcohol abuse">alcoholism</a>, and drug use are common causes of poor immune function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nutrition_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-nutrition-75">[75]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> is the most common cause of immunodeficiency in <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nutrition_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-nutrition-75">[75]</a></sup> Diets lacking sufficient protein are associated with impaired cell-mediated immunity, complement activity, phagocyte function, <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_A" title="Immunoglobulin A">IgA</a> antibody concentrations, and cytokine production. Additionally, the loss of the <a href="/wiki/Thymus" title="Thymus">thymus</a> at an early age through <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">genetic mutation</a> or surgical removal results in severe immunodeficiency and a high susceptibility to infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Immunodeficiencies can also be inherited or \'<a href="/wiki/Immunodeficiency#Acquired_immune_deficiency" title="Immunodeficiency">acquired\'</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberts_16-8"><a href="#cite_note-Alberts-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chronic_granulomatous_disease" title="Chronic granulomatous disease">Chronic granulomatous disease</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Phagocyte" title="Phagocyte">phagocytes</a> have a reduced ability to destroy pathogens, is an example of an inherited, or <a href="/wiki/Primary_immunodeficiency" title="Primary immunodeficiency">congenital, immunodeficiency</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIDS" title="AIDS">AIDS</a> and some types of <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> cause acquired immunodeficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Autoimmunity">Autoimmunity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Autoimmunity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Autoimmunity" title="Autoimmunity">Autoimmunity</a></div>\n<p>Overactive immune responses comprise the other end of immune dysfunction, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Autoimmunity" title="Autoimmunity">autoimmune disorders</a>. Here, the immune system fails to properly distinguish between self and non-self, and attacks part of the body. Under normal circumstances, many T cells and antibodies react with "self" peptides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> One of the functions of specialized cells (located in the <a href="/wiki/Thymus" title="Thymus">thymus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a>) is to present young lymphocytes with self antigens produced throughout the body and to eliminate those cells that recognize self-antigens, preventing autoimmunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sproul_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sproul-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypersensitivity">Hypersensitivity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Hypersensitivity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hypersensitivity" title="Hypersensitivity">Hypersensitivity</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Hypersensitivity" title="Hypersensitivity">Hypersensitivity</a> is an immune response that damages the body\'s own tissues. They are divided into four classes (Type I&#160;&#8211; IV) based on the mechanisms involved and the time course of the hypersensitive reaction. Type I hypersensitivity is an immediate or <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylactic</a> reaction, often associated with <a href="/wiki/Allergy" title="Allergy">allergy</a>. Symptoms can range from mild discomfort to death. Type I hypersensitivity is mediated by <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_E" title="Immunoglobulin E">IgE</a>, which triggers degranulation of <a href="/wiki/Mast_cell" title="Mast cell">mast cells</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basophil_granulocyte" title="Basophil granulocyte">basophils</a> when cross-linked by antigen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USCH_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-USCH-80">[80]</a></sup>\nType II hypersensitivity occurs when antibodies bind to antigens on the patient\'s own cells, marking them for destruction. This is also called antibody-dependent (or cytotoxic) hypersensitivity, and is mediated by <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_G" title="Immunoglobulin G">IgG</a> and <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_M" title="Immunoglobulin M">IgM</a> antibodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USCH_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-USCH-80">[80]</a></sup>\n<a href="/wiki/Immune_complex" title="Immune complex">Immune complexes</a> (aggregations of antigens, complement proteins, and IgG and IgM antibodies) deposited in various tissues trigger Type III hypersensitivity reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USCH_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-USCH-80">[80]</a></sup> Type IV hypersensitivity (also known as cell-mediated or <i>delayed type hypersensitivity</i>) usually takes between two and three days to develop. Type IV reactions are involved in many autoimmune and infectious diseases, but may also involve <i><a href="/wiki/Contact_dermatitis" title="Contact dermatitis">contact dermatitis</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poison_ivy" title="Poison ivy">poison ivy</a>). These reactions are mediated by <a href="/wiki/T_cell" title="T cell">T cells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monocyte" title="Monocyte">monocytes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USCH_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-USCH-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Idiopathic_inflammation">Idiopathic inflammation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Idiopathic inflammation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Immune-mediated_inflammatory_diseases" title="Immune-mediated inflammatory diseases">Immune-mediated inflammatory diseases</a></div>\n<p>Inflammation is one of the first responses of the immune system to infection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-31">[31]</a></sup> but it can appear without known cause.\n</p><p>Inflammation is produced by <a href="/wiki/Eicosanoid" title="Eicosanoid">eicosanoids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a>, which are released by injured or infected cells.
