Eicosanoids include <a href="/wiki/Prostaglandin" title="Prostaglandin">prostaglandins</a> that produce <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vasodilator" title="Vasodilator">dilation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a> associated with inflammation, and <a href="/wiki/Leukotriene" title="Leukotriene">leukotrienes</a> that attract certain <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cells</a> (leukocytes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-33">[33]</a></sup> Common cytokines include <a href="/wiki/Interleukin" title="Interleukin">interleukins</a> that are responsible for communication between white blood cells; <a href="/wiki/Chemokine" title="Chemokine">chemokines</a> that promote <a href="/wiki/Chemotaxis" title="Chemotaxis">chemotaxis</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Interferon" title="Interferon">interferons</a> that have <a href="/wiki/Antiviral_drug" title="Antiviral drug">anti-viral</a> effects, such as shutting down <a href="/wiki/Protein_biosynthesis" title="Protein biosynthesis">protein synthesis</a> in the host cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Growth_factor" title="Growth factor">Growth factors</a> and cytotoxic factors may also be released. These cytokines and other chemicals recruit immune cells to the site of infection and promote healing of any damaged tissue following the removal of pathogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_mechanisms_and_evolution">Other mechanisms and evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Other mechanisms and evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Innate_immune_system#Other_forms_of_innate_immunity" title="Innate immune system">Innate immune system &#167;&#160;Other forms of innate immunity</a></div>\n<p>It is likely that a multicomponent, adaptive immune system arose with the first <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> do not generate lymphocytes or an antibody-based humoral response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beck_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Beck-1">[1]</a></sup> Many species, however, utilize mechanisms that appear to be precursors of these aspects of vertebrate immunity. Immune systems appear even in the structurally most simple forms of life, with bacteria using a unique defense mechanism, called the <a href="/wiki/Restriction_modification_system" title="Restriction modification system">restriction modification system</a> to protect themselves from viral pathogens, called <a href="/wiki/Bacteriophage" title="Bacteriophage">bacteriophages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Prokaryotes also possess acquired immunity, through a system that uses <a href="/wiki/CRISPR" title="CRISPR">CRISPR</a> sequences to retain fragments of the genomes of phage that they have come into contact with in the past, which allows them to block virus replication through a form of <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Prokaryotes also possess other defense mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Offensive elements of the immune systems are also present in <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">unicellular eukaryotes</a>, but studies of their roles in defense are few.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pattern_recognition_receptor" title="Pattern recognition receptor">Pattern recognition receptors</a> are proteins used by nearly all organisms to identify molecules associated with pathogens. <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_peptides" title="Antimicrobial peptides">Antimicrobial peptides</a> called defensins are an evolutionarily conserved component of the innate immune response found in all animals and plants, and represent the main form of <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrate</a> systemic <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beck_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Beck-1">[1]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Complement_system" title="Complement system">complement system</a> and phagocytic cells are also used by most forms of invertebrate life. <a href="/wiki/Ribonuclease" title="Ribonuclease">Ribonucleases</a> and the <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a> pathway are conserved across all <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a>, and are thought to play a role in the immune response to viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unlike animals, plants lack phagocytic cells, but many plant immune responses involve systemic chemical signals that are sent through a plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plant_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plant-88">[88]</a></sup> Individual plant cells respond to molecules associated with pathogens known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pathogen-associated_molecular_patterns" title="Pathogen-associated molecular patterns">Pathogen-associated molecular patterns</a> or PAMPs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> When a part of a plant becomes infected, the plant produces a localized <a href="/wiki/Hypersensitive_response" title="Hypersensitive response">hypersensitive response</a>, whereby cells at the site of infection undergo rapid <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a> to prevent the spread of the disease to other parts of the plant. <a href="/wiki/Systemic_acquired_resistance" title="Systemic acquired resistance">Systemic acquired resistance</a> (SAR) is a type of defensive response used by plants that renders the entire plant <a href="/wiki/Disease_resistance_in_fruit_and_vegetables" title="Disease resistance in fruit and vegetables">resistant</a> to a particular infectious agent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plant_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plant-88">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA silencing</a> mechanisms are particularly important in this systemic response as they can block virus replication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tumor_immunology">Tumor immunology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Tumor immunology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cancer_immunology" title="Cancer immunology">Cancer immunology</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Macs_killing_cancer_cell.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1669" data-file-width="2289" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Macs_killing_cancer_cell.jpg/250px-Macs_killing_cancer_cell.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Macs_killing_cancer_cell.jpg/375px-Macs_killing_cancer_cell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Macs_killing_cancer_cell.jpg/500px-Macs_killing_cancer_cell.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Macs_killing_cancer_cell.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">Macrophages</a> have identified a cancer cell (the large, spiky mass). Upon fusing with the cancer cell, the macrophages (smaller white cells) inject toxins that kill the tumor cell. <a href="/wiki/Immunotherapy" title="Immunotherapy">Immunotherapy</a> for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Cancer#Immunotherapy" title="Cancer">cancer</a> is an active area of medical research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Another important role of the immune system is to identify and eliminate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor" title="Tumor">tumors</a>. This is called <b>immune surveillance</b>. The <i>transformed cells</i> of tumors express <a href="/wiki/Antigen#Tumor_antigens" title="Antigen">antigens</a> that are not found on normal cells. To the immune system, these antigens appear foreign, and their presence causes immune cells to attack the transformed tumor cells. The antigens expressed by tumors have several sources;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-anderson-92">[92]</a></sup> some are derived from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oncogenic" title="Oncogenic">oncogenic</a> viruses like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_papillomavirus" title="Human papillomavirus">human papillomavirus</a>, which causes <a href="/wiki/Cervical_cancer" title="Cervical cancer">cervical cancer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> while others are the organism\'s own proteins that occur at low levels in normal cells but reach high levels in tumor cells. One example is an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> called <a href="/wiki/Tyrosinase" title="Tyrosinase">tyrosinase</a> that, when expressed at high levels, transforms certain skin cells (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Melanocyte" title="Melanocyte">melanocytes</a>) into tumors called <a href="/wiki/Melanoma" title="Melanoma">melanomas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-romera_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-romera-95">[95]</a></sup> A third possible source of tumor antigens are proteins normally important for regulating <a href="/wiki/Cell_growth" title="Cell growth">cell growth</a> and survival, that commonly mutate into cancer inducing molecules called <a href="/wiki/Oncogene" title="Oncogene">oncogenes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-anderson-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guevara_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-guevara-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The main response of the immune system to tumors is to destroy the abnormal cells using killer T cells, sometimes with the assistance of helper T cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-romera_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-romera-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Tumor antigens are presented on MHC class I molecules in a similar way to viral antigens. This allows killer T cells to recognize the tumor cell as abnormal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seliger_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-seliger-99">[99]</a></sup> NK cells also kill tumorous cells in a similar way, especially if the tumor cells have fewer MHC class I molecules on their surface than normal; this is a common phenomenon with tumors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Sometimes antibodies are generated against tumor cells allowing for their destruction by the <a href="/wiki/Complement_system" title="Complement system">complement system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guevara_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-guevara-96">[96]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Clearly, some tumors evade the immune system and go on to become cancers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-selig_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-selig-102">[102]</a></sup> Tumor cells often have a reduced number of MHC class I molecules on their surface, thus avoiding detection by killer T cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seliger_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-seliger-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-101">[101]</a></sup> Some tumor cells also release products that inhibit the immune response; for example by secreting the cytokine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TGF_beta" title="TGF beta">TGF-&#946;</a>, which suppresses the activity of <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lymphocyte" title="Lymphocyte">lymphocytes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Immune_tolerance" title="Immune tolerance">immunological tolerance</a> may develop against tumor antigens, so the immune system no longer attacks the tumor cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_101-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-selig_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-selig-102">[102]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Paradoxically, macrophages can promote tumor growth <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> when tumor cells send out cytokines that attract macrophages, which then generate cytokines and growth factors such as <a href="/wiki/Tumor_necrosis_factor_alpha" title="Tumor necrosis factor alpha">tumor-necrosis factor alpha</a> that nurture tumor development or promote stem-cell-like plasticity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_101-4"><a href="#cite_note-:1-101">[101]</a></sup>  In addition, a combination of hypoxia in the tumor and a cytokine produced by macrophages induces tumor cells to decrease production of a protein that blocks <a href="/wiki/Metastasis" title="Metastasis">metastasis</a> and thereby assists spread of cancer cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_101-5"><a href="#cite_note-:1-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiological_regulation">Physiological regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Physiological regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The immune system is involved in many aspects of physiological regulation in the body. The immune system interacts intimately with other systems, such as the <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_system" title="Endocrine system">endocrine</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> systems. The immune system also plays a crucial role in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryogenesis" title="Embryogenesis">embryogenesis</a> (development of the embryo), as well as in <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissue</a> repair and <a href="/wiki/Regeneration_(biology)" title="Regeneration (biology)">regeneration</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hormones">Hormones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Hormones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">Hormones</a> can act as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunomodulators" title="Immunomodulators">immunomodulators</a>, altering the sensitivity of the immune system. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Female_sex_hormones" title="Female sex hormones">female sex hormones</a> are known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunostimulator" title="Immunostimulator">immunostimulators</a> of both adaptive<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> and innate immune responses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Some autoimmune diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Lupus_erythematosus" title="Lupus erythematosus">lupus erythematosus</a> strike women preferentially, and their onset often coincides with <a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">puberty</a>. By contrast, <a href="/wiki/Androgen" title="Androgen">male sex hormones</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Testosterone" title="Testosterone">testosterone</a> seem to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunosuppressive" title="Immunosuppressive">immunosuppressive</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Other hormones appear to regulate the immune system as well, most notably <a href="/wiki/Prolactin" title="Prolactin">prolactin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone" title="Growth hormone">growth hormone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">vitamin D</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vitamin_D">Vitamin D</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Vitamin D">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>When a T-cell encounters a foreign <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogen</a>, it extends a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vitamin_D_receptor" title="Vitamin D receptor">vitamin D receptor</a>. This is essentially a signaling device that allows the T-cell to bind to the active form of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">vitamin D</a>, the steroid hormone <a href="/wiki/Calcitriol" title="Calcitriol">calcitriol</a>. T-cells have a symbiotic relationship with vitamin D. Not only does the T-cell extend a vitamin D receptor, in essence asking to bind to the steroid hormone version of vitamin D, calcitriol, but the T-cell expresses the gene <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CYP27B1" title="CYP27B1">CYP27B1</a>, which is the gene responsible for converting the pre-hormone version of vitamin D, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcidiol" title="Calcidiol">calcidiol</a> into the steroid hormone version, calcitriol. Only after binding to calcitriol can T-cells perform their intended function. Other immune system cells that are known to express <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CYP27B1" title="CYP27B1">CYP27B1</a> and thus activate vitamin D calcidiol, are <a href="/wiki/Dendritic_cell" title="Dendritic cell">dendritic cells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Keratinocyte" title="Keratinocyte">keratinocytes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is conjectured that a progressive decline in hormone levels with age is partially responsible for weakened immune responses in aging individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Conversely, some hormones are regulated by the immune system, notably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyroid_hormone" title="Thyroid hormone">thyroid hormone</a> activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> The age-related decline in immune function is also related to decreasing vitamin D levels in the elderly. As people age, two things happen that negatively affect their vitamin D levels. First, they stay indoors more due to decreased activity levels. This means that they get less sun and therefore produce less <a href="/wiki/Cholecalciferol" title="Cholecalciferol">cholecalciferol</a> via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UVB_radiation" title="UVB radiation">UVB radiation</a>. Second, as a person ages the skin becomes less adept at producing vitamin D.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sleep_and_rest">Sleep and rest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Sleep and rest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The immune system is affected by sleep and rest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sleep_deprivation" title="Sleep deprivation">sleep deprivation</a> is detrimental to immune function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Complex feedback loops involving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cytokines" title="Cytokines">cytokines</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interleukin-1" title="Interleukin-1">interleukin-1</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor_necrosis_factor-%CE%B1" title="Tumor necrosis factor-&#945;">tumor necrosis factor-&#945;</a> produced in response to infection, appear to also play a role in the regulation of non-rapid eye movement (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/REM_sleep" title="REM sleep">REM</a>) sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Thus the immune response to infection may result in changes to the sleep cycle, including an increase in <a href="/wiki/Slow-wave_sleep" title="Slow-wave sleep">slow-wave sleep</a> relative to REM sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When suffering from sleep deprivation, active immunizations may have a diminished effect and may result in lower antibody production, and a lower immune response, than would be noted in a well-rested individual. Additionally, proteins such as <a href="/wiki/NFIL3" title="NFIL3">NFIL3</a>, which have been shown to be closely intertwined with both T-cell differentiation and our circadian rhythms, can be affected through the disturbance of natural light and dark cycles through instances of sleep deprivation, shift work, etc. As a result, these disruptions can lead to an increase in chronic conditions such as heart disease, chronic pain, and asthma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to the negative consequences of sleep deprivation, sleep and the intertwined circadian system have been shown to have strong regulatory effects on immunological functions affecting both the innate and the adaptive immunity. First, during the early slow-wave-sleep stage, a sudden drop in blood levels of <a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">cortisol</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epinephrine" title="Epinephrine">epinephrine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">norepinephrine</a> induce increased blood levels of the hormones leptin, pituitary growth hormone, and prolactin. These signals induce a pro-inflammatory state through the production of the pro-inflammatory cytokines interleukin-1, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interleukin-12" title="Interleukin-12">interleukin-12</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TNF-alpha" title="TNF-alpha">TNF-alpha</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IFN-gamma" title="IFN-gamma">IFN-gamma</a>. These cytokines then stimulate immune functions such as immune cells activation, proliferation, and differentiation. It is during this time that undifferentiated, or less differentiated, like na&#239;ve and central memory T cells, peak (i.e. during a time of a slowly evolving adaptive immune response).  In addition to these effects, the milieu of hormones produced at this time (leptin, pituitary growth hormone, and prolactin) support the interactions between APCs and T-cells, a shift of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Th1_cell" title="Th1 cell">T<sub>h</sub>1/T<sub>h</sub>2</a> cytokine balance towards one that supports T<sub>h</sub>1, an increase in overall T<sub>h</sub> cell proliferation, and na&#239;ve T cell migration to lymph nodes. This milieu is also thought to support the formation of long-lasting immune memory through the initiation of Th1 immune responses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sleep_and_immune_function_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sleep_and_immune_function-125">[125]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In contrast, during wake periods differentiated effector cells, such as cytotoxic natural killer cells and CTLs (cytotoxic T lymphocytes), peak in order to elicit an effective response against any intruding pathogens. As well during awake active times, anti-inflammatory molecules, such as cortisol and <a href="/wiki/Catecholamine" title="Catecholamine">catecholamines</a>, peak. There are two theories as to why the pro-inflammatory state is reserved for sleep time. First, inflammation would cause serious cognitive and physical impairments if it were to occur during wake times. Second, inflammation may occur during sleep times due to the presence of <a href="/wiki/Melatonin" title="Melatonin">melatonin</a>. Inflammation causes a great deal of <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_stress" title="Oxidative stress">oxidative stress</a> and the presence of melatonin during sleep times could actively counteract free radical production during this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sleep_and_immune_function_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sleep_and_immune_function-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition_and_diet">Nutrition and diet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Nutrition and diet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Overnutrition" title="Overnutrition">Overnutrition</a> is associated with diseases such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a>, which are known to affect immune function.  More moderate malnutrition, as well as certain specific trace mineral and nutrient deficiencies, can also compromise the immune response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Foods rich in certain <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a> may foster a healthy immune system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Likewise, <a href="/wiki/Prenatal_development" title="Prenatal development">fetal undernourishment</a> can cause a lifelong impairment of the immune system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Repair_and_regeneration">Repair and regeneration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Repair and regeneration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The immune system, particularly the innate component, plays a decisive role in <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissue</a> repair after an insult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-134">[134]</a></sup> Key actors include <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutrophil" title="Neutrophil">neutrophils</a>, but other cellular actors, including &#947;&#948; T cells, <a href="/wiki/Innate_lymphoid_cell" title="Innate lymphoid cell">innate lymphoid cells</a> (ILCs), and <a href="/wiki/Regulatory_T_cell" title="Regulatory T cell">regulatory T cells</a> (Tregs), are also important. The plasticity of immune cells and the balance between pro-inflammatory and anti-inflammatory signals are crucial aspects of efficient tissue repair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-134">[134]</a></sup> Immune components and pathways are involved in regeneration as well, for example in amphibians. According to one hypothesis, organisms that can regenerate could be less immunocompetent than organisms that cannot regenerate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Manipulation_in_medicine">Manipulation in medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Manipulation in medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dexamethasone_structure.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="407" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Dexamethasone_structure.svg/200px-Dexamethasone_structure.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Dexamethasone_structure.svg/300px-Dexamethasone_structure.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Dexamethasone_structure.svg/400px-Dexamethasone_structure.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dexamethasone_structure.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Immunosuppressive_drug" title="Immunosuppressive drug">immunosuppressive drug</a> <a href="/wiki/Dexamethasone" title="Dexamethasone">dexamethasone</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The immune response can be manipulated to suppress unwanted responses resulting from autoimmunity, allergy, and <a href="/wiki/Transplant_rejection" title="Transplant rejection">transplant rejection</a>, and to stimulate protective responses against pathogens that largely elude the immune system (see <a href="#Active_memory_and_immunization">immunization</a>) or cancer.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immunosuppression">Immunosuppression</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Immunosuppression">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Immunosuppressive_drug" title="Immunosuppressive drug">Immunosuppressive drugs</a> are used to control autoimmune disorders or <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> when excessive tissue damage occurs, and to prevent <a href="/wiki/Transplant_rejection" title="Transplant rejection">transplant rejection</a> after an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_transplant" title="Organ transplant">organ transplant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janeway6_38-6"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway6-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor-137">[137]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Anti-inflammatory" title="Anti-inflammatory">Anti-inflammatory</a> drugs are often used to control the effects of inflammation. <a href="/wiki/Glucocorticoid" title="Glucocorticoid">Glucocorticoids</a> are the most powerful of these drugs; however, these drugs can have many undesirable <a href="/wiki/Adverse_effect" title="Adverse effect">side effects</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_obesity" title="Central obesity">central obesity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyperglycemia" title="Hyperglycemia">hyperglycemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osteoporosis" title="Osteoporosis">osteoporosis</a>, and their use must be tightly controlled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Lower doses of anti-inflammatory drugs are often used in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxicity" title="Cytotoxicity">cytotoxic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Immunosuppressive_drug" title="Immunosuppressive drug">immunosuppressive drugs</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Methotrexate" title="Methotrexate">methotrexate</a> or <a href="/wiki/Azathioprine" title="Azathioprine">azathioprine</a>.\n<a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">Cytotoxic drugs</a> inhibit the immune response by killing dividing cells such as activated T cells. However, the killing is indiscriminate and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantly_dividing_cells" title="Constantly dividing cells">constantly dividing cells</a> and their organs are affected, which causes toxic side effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor-137">[137]</a></sup> Immunosuppressive drugs such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclosporin" title="Cyclosporin">cyclosporin</a> prevent T cells from responding to signals correctly by inhibiting <a href="/wiki/Signal_transduction" title="Signal transduction">signal transduction</a> pathways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immunostimulation">Immunostimulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Immunostimulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Immunotherapy" title="Immunotherapy">Immunotherapy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">Vaccination</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Cancer_immunotherapy" title="Cancer immunotherapy">Cancer immunotherapy</a> covers the medical ways to stimulate the immune system to attack cancer tumours.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theoretical_approaches_to_the_immune_system">Theoretical approaches to the immune system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Theoretical approaches to the immune system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Immunology is strongly experimental in everyday practice but is also characterized by an ongoing theoretical attitude. Many theories have been suggested in immunology from the end of the nineteenth century up to the present time. The end of the 19th century and the beginning of the 20th century saw a battle between "cellular" and "humoral" theories of immunity. According to the cellular theory of immunity, represented in particular by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elie_Metchnikoff" title="Elie Metchnikoff">Elie Metchnikoff</a>, it was cells&#8212;more precisely, phagocytes&#8212;that were responsible for immune responses. In contrast, the humoral theory of immunity, held, among others, by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Koch" title="Robert Koch">Robert Koch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emil_von_Behring" title="Emil von Behring">Emil von Behring</a>, stated that the active immune agents were soluble components (molecules) found in the organism&#8217;s &#8220;humors&#8221; rather than its cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-silverstein_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-silverstein-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tauber_AI_1994_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tauber_AI_1994-141">[141]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the mid-1950s, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Macfarlane_Burnet" title="Frank Macfarlane Burnet">Frank Burnet</a>, inspired by a suggestion made by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niels_Jerne" title="Niels Jerne">Niels Jerne</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> formulated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clonal_selection_theory" title="Clonal selection theory">clonal selection theory</a> (CST) of immunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> On the basis of CST, Burnet developed a theory of how an immune response is triggered according to the self/nonself distinction: "self" constituents (constituents of the body) do not trigger destructive immune responses, while "nonself" entities (pathogens, an allograft) trigger a destructive immune response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> The theory was later modified to reflect new discoveries regarding <a href="/wiki/Histocompatibility" title="Histocompatibility">histocompatibility</a> or the complex "two-signal" activation of T cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The self/nonself theory of immunity and the self/nonself vocabulary have been criticized,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tauber_AI_1994_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tauber_AI_1994-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matzinger_P_2002_301&#8211;5_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matzinger_P_2002_301&#8211;5-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> but remain very influential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>\n</p><p>More recently, several theoretical frameworks have been suggested in immunology, including "<a href="/wiki/Autopoiesis" title="Autopoiesis">autopoietic</a>" views,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid6235170_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid6235170-150">[150]</a></sup> "cognitive immune" views,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> the "<a href="/wiki/Danger_model" title="Danger model">danger model</a>" (or "danger theory"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matzinger_P_2002_301&#8211;5_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-Matzinger_P_2002_301&#8211;5-146">[146]</a></sup>  and the "discontinuity" theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> The danger model, suggested by <a href="/wiki/Polly_Matzinger" title="Polly Matzinger">Polly Matzinger</a> and colleagues, has been very influential, arousing many comments and discussions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Predicting_immunogenicity">Predicting immunogenicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Predicting immunogenicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Larger drugs (&gt;500 <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" title="Atomic mass unit">Da</a>) can provoke a neutralizing immune response, particularly if the drugs are administered repeatedly, or in larger doses. This limits the effectiveness of drugs based on larger peptides and proteins (which are typically larger than 6000 Da). In some cases, the drug itself is not immunogenic, but may be co-administered with an immunogenic compound, as is sometimes the case for <a href="/wiki/Paclitaxel" title="Paclitaxel">Taxol</a>. Computational methods have been developed to predict the immunogenicity of peptides and proteins, which are particularly useful in designing therapeutic antibodies, assessing likely virulence of mutations in viral coat particles, and validation of proposed peptide-based drug treatments. Early techniques relied mainly on the observation that <a href="/wiki/Hydrophile" title="Hydrophile">hydrophilic</a> <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> are overrepresented in <a href="/wiki/Epitope" title="Epitope">epitope</a> regions than <a href="/wiki/Hydrophobe" title="Hydrophobe">hydrophobic</a> amino acids;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Welling_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Welling-159">[159]</a></sup> however, more recent developments rely on <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a> techniques using databases of existing known epitopes, usually on well-studied virus proteins, as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Training_set" title="Training set">training set</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sollner_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sollner-160">[160]</a></sup> A publicly accessible database has been established for the cataloguing of epitopes from pathogens known to be recognizable by B cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saha_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saha-161">[161]</a></sup> The emerging field of <a href="/wiki/Bioinformatics" title="Bioinformatics">bioinformatics</a>-based studies of immunogenicity is referred to as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunoinformatics" title="Immunoinformatics">immunoinformatics</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flower_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flower-162">[162]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Immunoproteomics" title="Immunoproteomics">Immunoproteomics</a> is the study of large sets of proteins (<a href="/wiki/Proteomics" title="Proteomics">proteomics</a>) involved in the immune response.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Manipulation_by_pathogens">Manipulation by pathogens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Manipulation by pathogens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The success of any <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogen</a> depends on its ability to elude host immune responses. Therefore, pathogens evolved several methods that allow them to successfully infect a host, while evading detection or destruction by the immune system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finlay_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Finlay-163">[163]</a></sup> Bacteria often overcome physical barriers by secreting <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> that digest the barrier, for example, by using a <a href="/wiki/Type_II_secretion_system" title="Type II secretion system">type II secretion system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> Alternatively, using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_III_secretion_system" title="Type III secretion system">type III secretion system</a>, they may insert a hollow tube into the host cell, providing a direct route for proteins to move from the pathogen to the host. These proteins are often used to shut down host defenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>\n</p><p>An evasion strategy used by several pathogens to avoid the innate immune system is to hide within the cells of their host (also called <a href="/wiki/Intracellular" title="Intracellular">intracellular</a> <a href="/wiki/Pathogenesis" title="Pathogenesis">pathogenesis</a>). Here, a pathogen spends most of its <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">life-cycle</a> inside host cells, where it is shielded from direct contact with immune cells, antibodies and complement. Some examples of intracellular pathogens include viruses, the <a href="/wiki/Foodborne_illness" title="Foodborne illness">food poisoning</a> <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterium</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">Salmonella</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotic</a> parasites that cause <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_falciparum" title="Plasmodium falciparum">Plasmodium falciparum</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Leishmaniasis" title="Leishmaniasis">leishmaniasis</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Leishmania" title="Leishmania">Leishmania</a> spp.</i>). Other bacteria, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_tuberculosis" title="Mycobacterium tuberculosis">Mycobacterium tuberculosis</a></i>, live inside a protective capsule that prevents <a href="/wiki/Lysis" title="Lysis">lysis</a> by complement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> Many pathogens secrete compounds that diminish or misdirect the host\'s immune response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finlay_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-Finlay-163">[163]</a></sup> Some bacteria form <a href="/wiki/Biofilm" title="Biofilm">biofilms</a> to protect themselves from the cells and proteins of the immune system. Such biofilms are present in many successful infections, e.g., the chronic <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_aeruginosa" title="Pseudomonas aeruginosa">Pseudomonas aeruginosa</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Burkholderia_cenocepacia" title="Burkholderia cenocepacia">Burkholderia cenocepacia</a></i> infections characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Cystic_fibrosis" title="Cystic fibrosis">cystic fibrosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Other bacteria generate surface proteins that bind to antibodies, rendering them ineffective; examples include <i><a href="/wiki/Streptococcus" title="Streptococcus">Streptococcus</a></i> (protein G), <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Staphylococcus aureus">Staphylococcus aureus</a></i> (protein A), and <i><a href="/wiki/Peptostreptococcus" title="Peptostreptococcus">Peptostreptococcus</a> magnus</i> (protein L).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The mechanisms used to evade the adaptive immune system are more complicated. The simplest approach is to rapidly change non-essential <a href="/wiki/Epitope" title="Epitope">epitopes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> and/or sugars) on the surface of the pathogen, while keeping essential epitopes concealed. This is called <a href="/wiki/Antigenic_variation" title="Antigenic variation">antigenic variation</a>. An example is HIV, which mutates rapidly, so the proteins on its <a href="/wiki/Viral_envelope" title="Viral envelope">viral envelope</a> that are essential for entry into its host target cell are constantly changing. These frequent changes in antigens may explain the failures of <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a> directed at this virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> The parasite <i><a href="/wiki/Trypanosoma_brucei" title="Trypanosoma brucei">Trypanosoma brucei</a></i> uses a similar strategy, constantly switching one type of surface protein for another, allowing it to stay one step ahead of the antibody response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Masking antigens with host molecules is another common strategy for avoiding detection by the immune system. In HIV, the envelope that covers the <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">virion</a> is formed from the outermost membrane of the host cell; such "self-cloaked" viruses make it difficult for the immune system to identify them as "non-self" structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Immune_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cataphylaxis" title="Cataphylaxis">Cataphylaxis</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Clonal_selection" title="Clonal selection">Clonal selection</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hapten" title="Hapten">Hapten</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_physiology" title="Human physiology">Human physiology</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Immune_network_theory" title="Immune network theory">Immune network theory</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immune_system_receptors" title="Immune system receptors">Immune system receptors</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ImmunoGrid" title="ImmunoGrid">ImmunoGrid</a>, a project to model the mammalian, and specifically human, immune system using Grid technologies</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Immunoproteomics" title="Immunoproteomics">Immunoproteomics</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunostimulator" title="Immunostimulator">Immunostimulator</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Original_antigenic_sin" title="Original antigenic sin">Original antigenic sin</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Plant_disease_resistance" title="Plant disease resistance">Plant disease resistance</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyclonal_response" title="Polyclonal response">Polyclonal response</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Antigen#Tumor_antigens" title="Antigen">Tumor antigens</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Vaccine-naive" title="Vaccine-naive">Vaccine-naive</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mucosal_immunology" title="Mucosal immunology">Mucosal immunology</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline"
